	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Chinese language</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the official language of China and Singapore, also known as Mandarin, see <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>. For other languages spoken in China, see <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_China" title="Languages of China">Languages of China</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Han language" redirects here. For the Athabaskan language, see <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4n_language" title="Hän language">Hän language</a>. For the Koreanic language family, see <a href="/wiki/Han_languages" title="Han languages">Han languages</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Unless otherwise specified, Chinese in this article is written in <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified Chinese">simplified Chinese</a>/<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional Chinese">traditional Chinese</a>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">Pinyin</a> order. If the simplified and traditional characters are the same, they are written only once.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Chinese</b> (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hans">汉语</span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hant">漢語</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>&#58; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Hànyǔ</span></i>; literally&#58; '<a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han</a> language'; or especially though not exclusively for written Chinese: <span lang="zh">中文</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Zhōngwén</span></i>; 'Chinese writing') is a group of related, but in many cases not <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually intelligible</a>, language <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">varieties</a>, forming the <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">Sinitic</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_language_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Sino-Tibetan language family">Sino-Tibetan language family</a>. Chinese is spoken by the ethnic <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Chinese</a> majority and many minority ethnic groups in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. About 1.2 billion people (around 16% of the world's population) speak some form of Chinese as their <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a> are usually described by native speakers as dialects of a single Chinese language, but linguists note that they are as diverse as a <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language family</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> The internal diversity of Chinese has been likened to that of the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a> but may be even more varied. There are between 7 and 13 main regional groups of Chinese (depending on classification scheme), of which the most spoken by far is <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> (about 800 million, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Mandarin" title="Southwestern Mandarin">Southwestern Mandarin</a>), followed by <a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min</a> (75 million, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Southern_Min" title="Southern Min">Southern Min</a>), <a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu</a> (74 million, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>), <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue</a> (68 million, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>), etc.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChinese_Academy_of_Social_Sciences20123,_125_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChinese_Academy_of_Social_Sciences20123,_125-6">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Most of these groups are mutually unintelligible, and even dialect groups within Min Chinese may not be mutually intelligible. Some, however, like <a href="/wiki/Xiang_Chinese" title="Xiang Chinese">Xiang</a> and certain <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Southwest Mandarin">Southwest Mandarin</a> dialects, may share common terms and a certain degree of intelligibility. All varieties of Chinese are <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tonal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytic</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> <i>(Pǔtōnghuà/Guóyǔ/Huáyǔ)</i> is a standardized form of spoken Chinese based on the <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">Beijing dialect</a> of Mandarin. It is the official language of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, a national language of <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and one of the four official languages of <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>. It is one of the <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_the_United_Nations" title="Official languages of the United Nations">six official languages</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Written_Chinese" title="Written Chinese">written form</a> of the standard language (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">中文</span>; <i>Zhōngwén</i>), based on the <a href="/wiki/Logograms" class="mw-redirect" title="Logograms">logograms</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> (<span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">汉字</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">漢字</span>; <i>Hànzì</i>), is shared by literate speakers of otherwise unintelligible dialects.
</p><p>The earliest Chinese written records are <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>-era oracle inscriptions, which can be traced back to 1250 BCE. The phonetic categories of <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaic Chinese">Archaic Chinese</a> can be reconstructed from the rhymes of ancient poetry. During the <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Northern and Southern dynasties</a> period, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> went through several <a href="/wiki/Sound_changes" class="mw-redirect" title="Sound changes">sound changes</a> and split into several varieties following prolonged geographic and political separation. <i><a href="/wiki/Qieyun" title="Qieyun">Qieyun</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Rime_dictionary" title="Rime dictionary">rime dictionary</a>, recorded a compromise between the pronunciations of different regions. The royal courts of the Ming and early Qing dynasties operated using a <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koiné language</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(late_imperial_lingua_franca)" title="Mandarin (late imperial lingua franca)">Guanhua</a>) based on <a href="/wiki/Nanjing_dialect" title="Nanjing dialect">Nanjing dialect</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin" title="Lower Yangtze Mandarin">Lower Yangtze Mandarin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> was adopted in the 1930s, and is now the official language of both the People's Republic of China and the Republic of China on Taiwan.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Linguists classify all varieties of Chinese as part of the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_language_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Sino-Tibetan language family">Sino-Tibetan language family</a>, together with <a href="/wiki/Burmese_language" title="Burmese language">Burmese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Tibetan languages">Tibetan</a> and many other languages spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asian_Massif" title="Southeast Asian Massif">Southeast Asian Massif</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198812–13_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198812–13-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Although the relationship was first proposed in the early 19th century and is now broadly accepted, reconstruction of Sino-Tibetan is much less developed than that of families such as <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> or <a href="/wiki/Austroasiatic_languages" title="Austroasiatic languages">Austroasiatic</a>. Difficulties have included the great diversity of the languages, the lack of <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflection</a> in many of them, and the effects of language contact. In addition, many of the smaller languages are spoken in mountainous areas that are difficult to reach, and are often also sensitive <a href="/wiki/Border_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Border zone">border zones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHandel2008422,_434–436_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHandel2008422,_434–436-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Without a secure reconstruction of proto-Sino-Tibetan, the higher-level structure of the family remains unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHandel2008426_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHandel2008426-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> A top-level branching into Chinese and <a href="/wiki/Tibeto-Burman_languages" title="Tibeto-Burman languages">Tibeto-Burman languages</a> is often assumed, but has not been convincingly demonstrated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHandel2008431_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHandel2008431-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Chinese_language" title="History of the Chinese language">History of the Chinese language</a></div>
<p>The first written records appeared over 3,000 years ago during the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>. As the language evolved over this period, the various local varieties became mutually unintelligible. In reaction, central governments have repeatedly sought to promulgate a unified standard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988183–185_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988183–185-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_and_Middle_Chinese">Old and Middle Chinese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Old and Middle Chinese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest examples of Chinese are divinatory inscriptions on <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone" title="Oracle bone">oracle bones</a> from around 1250 BCE in the late <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuessler20071_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuessler20071-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> was the language of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou</a> period (1046–771 BCE), recorded in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">inscriptions on bronze artifacts</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Poetry" title="Classic of Poetry">Classic of Poetry</a></i> and portions of the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Documents" title="Book of Documents">Book of Documents</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter19922–3_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter19922–3-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Scholars have attempted to reconstruct the <a href="/wiki/Phonology_of_Old_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonology of Old Chinese">phonology of Old Chinese</a> by comparing later varieties of Chinese with the rhyming practice of the <i>Classic of Poetry</i> and the phonetic elements found in the majority of Chinese characters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198842–45_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198842–45-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Although many of the finer details remain unclear, most scholars agree that Old Chinese differs from Middle Chinese in lacking retroflex and palatal obstruents but having initial <a href="/wiki/Consonant_cluster" title="Consonant cluster">consonant clusters</a> of some sort, and in having voiceless nasals and liquids.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992177_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992177-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Most recent reconstructions also describe an atonal language with consonant clusters at the end of the syllable, developing into <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a> distinctions in Middle Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992181–183_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992181–183-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>  Several <a href="/wiki/Derivational_affix" class="mw-redirect" title="Derivational affix">derivational affixes</a> have also been identified, but the language lacks <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflection</a>, and indicated grammatical relationships using word order and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_particle" title="Grammatical particle">grammatical particles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuessler200712_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuessler200712-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> was the language used during <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Northern and Southern dynasties</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song</a> dynasties (6th through 10th centuries CE). It can be divided into an early period, reflected by the <i><a href="/wiki/Qieyun" title="Qieyun">Qieyun</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Rime_dictionary" title="Rime dictionary">rime book</a> (601 CE), and a late period in the 10th century, reflected by <a href="/wiki/Rhyme_table" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhyme table">rhyme tables</a> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Yunjing" title="Yunjing">Yunjing</a></i> constructed by ancient Chinese philologists as a guide to the <i>Qieyun</i> system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter199214–15_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter199214–15-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> These works define phonological categories, but with little hint of what sounds they represent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey1987125_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey1987125-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Linguists have identified these sounds by comparing the categories with pronunciations in modern <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sino-Xenic_vocabularies" class="mw-redirect" title="Sino-Xenic vocabularies">borrowed Chinese words</a> in Japanese, Vietnamese, and Korean, and transcription evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198834–42_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198834–42-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The resulting system is very complex, with a large number of consonants and vowels, but they are probably not all distinguished in any single dialect. Most linguists now believe it represents a <a href="/wiki/Diasystem" title="Diasystem">diasystem</a> encompassing 6th-century northern and southern standards for reading the classics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198824_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198824-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_and_literary_forms">Classical and literary forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classical and literary forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a></div>
<p>The relationship between spoken and written Chinese is rather complex. Its spoken varieties have evolved at different rates, while written Chinese itself has changed much less. <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a> <a href="/wiki/Literature" title="Literature">literature</a> began in the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_northern_dialects">Rise of northern dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Rise of northern dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Song" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Song">Northern Song</a> dynasty, and during the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin</a> (Jurchen) and <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan</a> (Mongol) dynasties in northern China, a common speech (now called <a href="/wiki/Old_Mandarin" title="Old Mandarin">Old Mandarin</a>) developed based on the dialects of the <a href="/wiki/North_China_Plain" title="North China Plain">North China Plain</a> around the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198848_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198848-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Zhongyuan_Yinyun" title="Zhongyuan Yinyun">Zhongyuan Yinyun</a></i> (1324) was a dictionary that codified the rhyming conventions of new <i><a href="/wiki/Sanqu" title="Sanqu">sanqu</a></i> verse form in this language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198848–49_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198848–49-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
Together with the slightly later <i><a href="/wiki/Menggu_Ziyun" title="Menggu Ziyun">Menggu Ziyun</a></i>, this dictionary describes a language with many of the features characteristic of modern <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_dialects" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandarin dialects">Mandarin dialects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198849–51_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198849–51-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Up to the early 20th century, most of the people in China spoke only their local variety.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988133,_247_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988133,_247-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
As a practical measure, officials of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing</a> dynasties carried out the administration of the empire using a <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(late_imperial_lingua_franca)" title="Mandarin (late imperial lingua franca)">common language based on Mandarin varieties</a>, known as <i>Guānhuà</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">官话</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">官話</span>, literally "language of officials").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988136_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988136-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
For most of this period, this language was a <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koiné</a> based on dialects spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> area, though not identical to any single dialect.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2000549–550_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2000549–550-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
By the middle of the 19th century, the Beijing dialect had become dominant and was essential for any business with the imperial court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2000540–541_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2000540–541-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1930s a <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">standard national language</a> <i>Guóyǔ</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">国语</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">國語</span> "national language") was adopted.
After much dispute between proponents of northern and southern dialects and an abortive attempt at an artificial pronunciation, the <a href="/wiki/National_Languages_Committee" title="National Languages Committee">National Language Unification Commission</a> finally settled on the Beijing dialect in 1932. The People's Republic founded in 1949 retained this standard, calling it <i>pǔtōnghuà</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">普通话</span>/<span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">普通話</span> "common speech").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey19873–15_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey19873–15-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The national language is now used in education, the media, and formal situations in both Mainland China and Taiwan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988133_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988133-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>, because of their colonial and linguistic history, the language used in education, the media, formal speech, and everyday life remains the local <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>, although the standard language has become very influential and is being taught in schools.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhangYang2004_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhangYang2004-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Adoption_of_Chinese_literary_culture" title="Adoption of Chinese literary culture">Adoption of Chinese literary culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sino-Xenic_vocabularies" class="mw-redirect" title="Sino-Xenic vocabularies">Sino-Xenic vocabularies</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg/220px-Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="189" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg/330px-Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg/440px-Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="1163" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tripitaka_Koreana.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Tripitaka_Koreana" title="Tripitaka Koreana">Tripitaka Koreana</a></i>, a Korean collection of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhist_canon" title="Chinese Buddhist canon">Chinese Buddhist canon</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Chinese language has spread to neighbouring countries through a variety of means. Northern Vietnam was incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/Han_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Han empire">Han empire</a> in 111 BCE, marking the beginning of a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_domination_of_Vietnam" title="Chinese domination of Vietnam">period of Chinese control</a> that ran almost continuously for a millennium. The <a href="/wiki/Four_Commanderies_of_Han" title="Four Commanderies of Han">Four Commanderies</a> were established in northern Korea in the first century BCE, but disintegrated in the following centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESohnLee200323_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESohnLee200323-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Chinese Buddhism</a> spread over East Asia between the 2nd and 5th centuries CE, and with it the study of scriptures and literature in <a href="/wiki/Literary_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Literary Chinese">Literary Chinese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller196729–30_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller196729–30-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Later Korea, Japan, and Vietnam developed strong central governments modeled on Chinese institutions, with Literary Chinese as the language of administration and scholarship, a position it would retain until the late 19th century in Korea and (to a lesser extent) Japan, and the early 20th century in Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKornicki201175–77_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKornicki201175–77-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Scholars from different lands could communicate, albeit only in writing, using Literary Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKornicki201167_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKornicki201167-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although they used Chinese solely for written communication, each country had its own tradition of reading texts aloud, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Sino-Xenic_pronunciations" title="Sino-Xenic pronunciations">Sino-Xenic pronunciations</a>. Chinese words with these pronunciations were also extensively imported into the <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a> languages, and today comprise over half of their vocabularies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiyake200498–99_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiyake200498–99-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This massive influx led to changes in the phonological structure of the languages, contributing to the development of <a href="/wiki/Mora_(linguistics)" title="Mora (linguistics)">moraic</a> structure in Japanese<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990120–121_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990120–121-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and the disruption of <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">vowel harmony</a> in Korean.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESohn200189_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESohn200189-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Borrowed Chinese morphemes have been used extensively in all these languages to coin compound words for new concepts, in a similar way to the use of <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> roots in European languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990146_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990146-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Many new compounds, or new meanings for old phrases, were created in the late 19th and early 20th centuries to name Western concepts and artifacts. These coinages, written in shared Chinese characters, have then been borrowed freely between languages. They have even been accepted into Chinese, a language usually resistant to loanwords, because their foreign origin was hidden by their written form. Often different compounds for the same concept were in circulation for some time before a winner emerged, and sometimes the final choice differed between countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200043_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200043-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The proportion of vocabulary of Chinese origin thus tends to be greater in technical, abstract, or formal language. For example, in Japan, <a href="/wiki/Sino-Japanese_vocabulary" title="Sino-Japanese vocabulary">Sino-Japanese words</a> account for about 35% of the words in entertainment magazines, over half the words in newspapers, and 60% of the words in science magazines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990143_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShibatani1990143-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Vietnam, Korea, and Japan each developed writing systems for their own languages, initially based on <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, but later replaced with the <i><a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">Hangul</a></i> alphabet for Korean and supplemented with <i><a href="/wiki/Kana" title="Kana">kana</a></i> syllabaries for Japanese, while Vietnamese continued to be written with the complex <i><a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_n%C3%B4m" class="mw-redirect" title="Chữ nôm">Chữ nôm</a></i> script. However, these were limited to popular literature until the late 19th century. Today Japanese is written with a composite script using both Chinese characters (<i><a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">Kanji</a></i>) and kana. Korean is written exclusively with Hangul in North Korea, and supplementary Chinese characters (<i><a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a></i>) are increasingly rarely used in South Korea. Vietnamese is written with <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_alphabet" title="Vietnamese alphabet">a Latin-based alphabet</a>.
</p><p>Examples of <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_words_of_Chinese_origin" title="List of English words of Chinese origin">loan words in English</a> include "<a href="/wiki/Tea" title="Tea">tea</a>", from <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> (Min Nan) <i><span lang="nan-Latn"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/t%C3%AA" class="extiw" title="wikt:tê">tê</a></span></i> (<span lang="zh"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%8C%B6" class="extiw" title="wikt:茶">茶</a></span>), "<a href="/wiki/Dim_sum" title="Dim sum">dim sum</a>", from Cantonese <i>dim<sup>2</sup> sam<sup>1</sup></i> and "<a href="/wiki/Kumquat" title="Kumquat">kumquat</a>", from Cantonese <i>gam<sup>1</sup>gwat<sup>1</sup></i> (<span lang="zh"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%87%91%E6%A9%98" class="extiw" title="wikt:金橘">金橘</a></span>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Varieties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">Varieties of Chinese</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg/440px-Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="440" height="349" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg/660px-Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg/880px-Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2219" data-file-height="1759" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_sinitic_languages_full-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Range of Chinese dialect groups according to the <i>Language Atlas of China</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jerry_Norman_(sinologist)" title="Jerry Norman (sinologist)">Jerry Norman</a> estimated that there are hundreds of mutually unintelligible varieties of Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman200372_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman200372-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> These varieties form a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>, in which differences in speech generally become more pronounced as distances increase, though the rate of change varies immensely.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988189–190_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988189–190-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Generally, mountainous South China exhibits more linguistic diversity than the <a href="/wiki/North_China_Plain" title="North China Plain">North China Plain</a>. In parts of South China, a major city's dialect may only be marginally intelligible to close neighbors. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Wuzhou" title="Wuzhou">Wuzhou</a> is about 120 miles (190&#160;km) upstream from <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue</a> variety spoken there is more like that of Guangzhou than is that of <a href="/wiki/Taishan,_Guangdong" title="Taishan, Guangdong">Taishan</a>, 60 miles (95&#160;km) southwest of Guangzhou and separated from it by several rivers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198723_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198723-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In parts of <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> the speech of neighboring counties or even villages may be mutually unintelligible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988188_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988188-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the late 20th century, Chinese emigrants to Southeast Asia and North America came from southeast coastal areas, where Min, Hakka, and Yue dialects are spoken.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988191_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988191-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
The vast majority of Chinese immigrants to North America spoke the <a href="/wiki/Taishan_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Taishan dialect">Taishan dialect</a>, from a small coastal area southwest of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198798_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198798-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grouping">Grouping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Grouping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>Proportions of first-language speakers<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChinese_Academy_of_Social_Sciences20123,_125_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChinese_Academy_of_Social_Sciences20123,_125-6">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ac8761; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> (65.7%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #b1bab6; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min</a> (6.2%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #c8c8a8; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu</a> (6.1%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #bf6e7b; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue</a> (5.6%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #96642c; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">Jin</a> (5.2%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fbd98d; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">Gan</a> (3.9%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #eeb36a; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a> (3.5%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #a5ae87; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Xiang_Chinese" title="Xiang Chinese">Xiang</a> (3.0%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #9b987f; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Huizhou_Chinese" title="Huizhou Chinese">Huizhou</a> (0.3%)</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #946565; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pinghua" title="Pinghua">Pinghua</a>, others (0.6%)</div>
</div>
</div></div>
<p>Local varieties of Chinese are conventionally classified into seven dialect groups, largely on the basis of the different evolution of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> voiced initials:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988181_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988181-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201053–55_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201053–55-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">Pekinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sichuanese_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Sichuanese Mandarin">Sichuanese</a>, and also the <a href="/wiki/Dungan_language" title="Dungan language">Dungan language</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Shanghainese dialect">Shanghainese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzhou_dialect" title="Suzhou dialect">Suzhounese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wenzhounese" title="Wenzhounese">Wenzhounese</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">Gan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Xiang_Chinese" title="Xiang Chinese">Xiang</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">Min</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Fuzhou_dialect" title="Fuzhou dialect">Fuzhounese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hainanese" title="Hainanese">Hainanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teochew_dialect" title="Teochew dialect">Teochew</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taishanese" title="Taishanese">Taishanese</a></li></ul>
<p>The classification of <a href="/wiki/Li_Rong_(linguist)" title="Li Rong (linguist)">Li Rong</a>, which is used in the <i><a href="/wiki/Language_Atlas_of_China" title="Language Atlas of China">Language Atlas of China</a></i> (1987), distinguishes three further groups:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201055–56_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201055–56-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">Jin</a>, previously included in Mandarin.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Huizhou_Chinese" title="Huizhou Chinese">Huizhou</a>, previously included in Wu.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pinghua_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinghua Chinese">Pinghua</a>, previously included in Yue.</li></ul>
<p>Some varieties remain unclassified, including <a href="/wiki/Danzhou_dialect" title="Danzhou dialect">Danzhou dialect</a> (spoken in <a href="/wiki/Danzhou" title="Danzhou">Danzhou</a>, on <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">Hainan</a> Island), <a href="/wiki/Waxianghua" class="mw-redirect" title="Waxianghua">Waxianghua</a> (spoken in western <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Shaozhou_Tuhua" title="Shaozhou Tuhua">Shaozhou Tuhua</a> (spoken in northern <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201072–73_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201072–73-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_Chinese">Standard Chinese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Standard Chinese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_territorial_entities_where_Chinese_is_an_official_language" title="List of territorial entities where Chinese is an official language">List of territorial entities where Chinese is an official language</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>, often called Mandarin, is the official <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">standard language</a> of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, and one of the four official languages of <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> (where it is called "Huáyŭ" <span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">华语</span> or simply Chinese). Standard Chinese is based on the <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">Beijing dialect</a>, the dialect of <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> as spoken in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>. The governments of both China and Taiwan intend for speakers of all Chinese speech varieties to use it as a common language of communication. Therefore, it is used in government agencies, in the media, and as a language of instruction in schools.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a> and Taiwan, <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a> has been a common feature. For example, in addition to Standard Chinese, a resident of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> might speak <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>; and, if he or she grew up elsewhere, then he or she is also likely to be fluent in the particular dialect of that local area. A native of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> may speak both Cantonese and Standard Chinese. In addition to Mandarin, most <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_people" title="Taiwanese people">Taiwanese</a> also speak <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Minnan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hakka_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Hakka language">Hakka</a>, or an <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> A Taiwanese may commonly mix pronunciations, phrases, and words from Mandarin and other <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Taiwan" title="Languages of Taiwan">Taiwanese languages</a>, and this mixture is considered normal in daily or informal speech.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The official Chinese designation for the major branches of Chinese is <i>fāngyán</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">方言</span>, literally "regional speech"), whereas the more closely related varieties within these are called <i>dìdiǎn fāngyán</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">地点方言</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">地點方言</span> "local speech").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198457_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198457-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Conventional English-language usage in Chinese linguistics is to use <i>dialect</i> for the speech of a particular place (regardless of status) and <i>dialect group</i> for a regional grouping such as Mandarin or Wu.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman200372_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman200372-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Because varieties from different groups are not <a href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually intelligible">mutually intelligible</a>, some scholars prefer to describe Wu and others as separate languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomason198827–28_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomason198827–28-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs a source specifically saying that scholars do this and for this reason (June 2015)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Norman_(sinologist)" title="Jerry Norman (sinologist)">Jerry Norman</a> called this practice misleading, pointing out that Wu, which itself contains many mutually unintelligible varieties, could not be properly called a single language under the same criterion, and that the same is true for each of the other groups.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman200372_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman200372-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mutual intelligibility is considered by some linguists to be the main criterion for determining whether varieties are separate languages or dialects of a single language,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair199117_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair199117-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> although others do not regard it as decisive,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198454_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198454-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomaine200013,_23_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomaine200013,_23-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWardaughFuller201428–32_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWardaughFuller201428–32-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiang201411–14_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiang201411–14-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHymes197164_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHymes197164-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> particularly when cultural factors interfere as they do with Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomason198827_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomason198827-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> As <a href="#CITEREFCampbell2008">Campbell (2008)</a> explains, linguists often ignore mutual intelligibility when varieties share intelligibility with a central variety (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Prestige_variety" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestige variety">prestige variety</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Mandarin">Standard Mandarin</a>), as the issue requires some careful handling when mutual intelligibility is inconsistent with language identity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell2008637_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell2008637-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_DeFrancis" title="John DeFrancis">John DeFrancis</a> argues that it is inappropriate to refer to Mandarin, Wu and so on as "dialects" because the mutual unintelligibility between them is too great. On the other hand, he also objects to considering them as separate languages, as it incorrectly implies a set of disruptive "religious, economic, political, and other differences" between speakers that exist, for example, between <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_Canada" title="French language in Canada">French Catholics</a> and English Protestants in Canada, but not between speakers of Cantonese and Mandarin in China, owing to China's near-uninterrupted history of centralized government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198455–57_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198455–57-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the difficulties involved in determining the difference between language and dialect, other terms have been proposed. These include <i>vernacular</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaugen1966927_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaugen1966927-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Lect" class="mw-redirect" title="Lect">lect</a></i> <sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <i>regionalect</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198457_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis198457-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> <i>topolect</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair19917_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair19917-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Language_variety" class="mw-redirect" title="Language variety">variety</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHudson199622_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHudson199622-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Chinese people consider the spoken varieties as one single language because speakers share a common culture and history, as well as a shared national identity and a common written form.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter19927–8_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter19927–8-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> To Chinese nationalists, the idea of Chinese as a language family may suggest that the Chinese identity is much more fragmented and disunified than it actually is and as such is often looked upon as culturally and politically provocative. Additionally, <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> it is closely associated with <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_independence" class="mw-redirect" title="Taiwanese independence">Taiwanese independence</a>,</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> some of whose supporters promote the local <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese Hokkien</a> variety.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese_phonology" title="Standard Chinese phonology">Standard Chinese phonology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Historical_Chinese_phonology" title="Historical Chinese phonology">Historical Chinese phonology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese#Phonology" title="Varieties of Chinese">Varieties of Chinese §&#160;Phonology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="29.28" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/27/Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav/Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="95072" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/27/Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav/Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="170328" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav" type="audio/wav" data-title="Original WAV file (768 kbps)" data-shorttitle="WAV source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="768000" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edmund_Yeo_-_voice_-_ch_150127_1828.wav" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spoken <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin Chinese</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonological</a> structure of each syllable consists of a <a href="/wiki/Syllable_nucleus" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable nucleus">nucleus</a> that has a <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowel</a> (which can be a <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthong</a>, or even a <a href="/wiki/Triphthong" title="Triphthong">triphthong</a> in certain varieties), preceded by an <a href="/wiki/Syllable_onset" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable onset">onset</a> (a single <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonant</a>, or consonant+<a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">glide</a>; zero onset is also possible), and followed (optionally) by a <a href="/wiki/Syllable_coda" class="mw-redirect" title="Syllable coda">coda</a> consonant; a syllable also carries a <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a>. There are some instances where a vowel is not used as a nucleus. An example of this is in <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Nasal_stop" class="mw-redirect" title="Nasal stop">nasal</a> <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorant</a> consonants <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/m/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ŋ/</span> can stand alone as their own syllable.
</p><p>In Mandarin much more than in other spoken varieties, most syllables tend to be open syllables, meaning they have no coda (assuming that a final <a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">glide</a> is not analyzed as a coda), but syllables that do have codas are restricted to nasals <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/m/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/n/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ŋ/</span>, the retroflex approximant <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɻ /</span>, and voiceless stops <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/p/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/k/</span>, or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ʔ/</span>. Some varieties allow most of these codas, whereas others, such as <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>, are limited to only <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/n/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ŋ/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/ɻ /</span>.
</p><p>The number of sounds in the different spoken dialects varies, but in general there has been a tendency to a reduction in sounds from <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>. The Mandarin dialects in particular have experienced a dramatic decrease in sounds and so have far more multisyllabic words than most other spoken varieties. The total number of syllables in some varieties is therefore only about a thousand, including tonal variation, which is only about an eighth as many as English.<sup id="cite_ref-DeFrancis_p42_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeFrancis_p42-71">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tones">Tones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Tones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All varieties of spoken Chinese use <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tones</a> to distinguish words.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198852_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198852-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chao_p24_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chao_p24-74">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup> A few dialects of north China may have as few as three tones, while some dialects in south China have up to 6 or 12 tones, depending on how one counts. One exception from this is <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Shanghainese dialect">Shanghainese</a> which has reduced the set of tones to a two-toned <a href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" class="mw-redirect" title="Pitch accent">pitch accent</a> system much like modern Japanese.
</p><p>A very common example used to illustrate the use of tones in Chinese is the application of the four tones of <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> (along with the neutral tone) to the syllable <i>ma</i>. The tones are exemplified by the following five Chinese words:
</p>

<td><span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%AA%82" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:骂">骂</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%BD%B5" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:罵">罵</a></span>
