<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_grammar" title="Chinese grammar">Chinese grammar</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classifiers" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese classifiers">Chinese classifiers</a></div>
<p>Chinese is often described as a "monosyllabic" language. However, this is only partially correct. It is largely accurate when describing <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>; in Classical Chinese, for example, perhaps 90% of words correspond to a single syllable and a single character. In the modern varieties, it is usually the case that a <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a> (unit of meaning) is a single syllable; In contrast, English has plenty of multi-syllable morphemes, both bound and free, such as "seven", "elephant", "para-" and "-able".
</p><p>Some of the conservative southern varieties of modern Chinese have largely monosyllabic words, especially among the more basic vocabulary. In modern Mandarin, however, most <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verbs</a> are largely disyllabic. A significant cause of this is <a href="/wiki/Phonetic_erosion" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonetic erosion">phonological attrition</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sound_change" title="Sound change">Sound change</a> over time has steadily reduced the number of possible syllables. In modern Mandarin, there are now only about 1,200 possible syllables, including tonal distinctions, compared with about 5,000 in <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a> (still largely monosyllabic) and over 8,000 in English.<sup id="cite_ref-DeFrancis_p42_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeFrancis_p42-71">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This phonological collapse has led to a corresponding increase in the number of <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">homophones</a>. As an example, the small Langenscheidt Pocket Chinese Dictionary<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> lists six words that are commonly pronounced as <i>shí</i> (tone 2): <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">十</span> "ten"; <span lang="zh-Hans">实</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">實</span> "real, actual"; <span lang="zh-Hans">识</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">識</span> "know (a person), recognize"; <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">石</span> "stone"; <span lang="zh-Hans">时</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">時</span> "time"; <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">食</span> "food, eat". These were all pronounced differently in <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Chinese" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Middle Chinese">Early Middle Chinese</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Baxter%27s_transcription_for_Middle_Chinese" title="Baxter&#39;s transcription for Middle Chinese">William H. Baxter's transcription</a> they were <i>dzyip</i>, <i>zyit</i>, <i>syik</i>, <i>dzyek</i>, <i>dzyi</i> and <i>zyik</i> respectively. They are still pronounced differently in today's <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Jyutping" title="Jyutping">Jyutping</a> they are <i>sap9</i>, <i>sat9</i>, <i>sik7</i>, <i>sek9</i>, <i>si4</i>, <i>sik9</i>. In modern spoken Mandarin, however, tremendous ambiguity would result if all of these words could be used as-is; <a href="/wiki/Yuen_Ren_Chao" title="Yuen Ren Chao">Yuen Ren Chao</a>'s modern poem <a href="/wiki/Lion-Eating_Poet_in_the_Stone_Den" title="Lion-Eating Poet in the Stone Den">Lion-Eating Poet in the Stone Den</a> exploits this, consisting of 92 characters all pronounced <i>shi</i>. As such, most of these words have been replaced (in speech, if not in writing) with a longer, less-ambiguous compound. Only the first one, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">十</span> "ten", normally appears as such when spoken; the rest are normally replaced with, respectively, <i>shíjì</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">实际</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">實際</span> (lit. "actual-connection"); <i>rènshi</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">认识</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">認識</span> (lit. "recognize-know"); <i>shítou</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">石头</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">石頭</span> (lit. "stone-head"); <i>shíjiān</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">时间</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">時間</span> (lit. "time-interval"); <i>shíwù</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">食物</span> (lit. "food-thing"). In each case, the homophone was disambiguated by adding another morpheme, typically either a synonym or a generic word of some sort (for example, "head", "thing"), the purpose of which is simply to indicate which of the possible meanings of the other, homophonic syllable should be selected.
</p><p>However, when one of the above words forms part of a compound, the disambiguating syllable is generally dropped and the resulting word is still disyllabic. For example, <i>shí</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">石</span> alone, not <i>shítou</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">石头</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">石頭</span>, appears in compounds meaning "stone-", for example, <i>shígāo</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">石膏</span> "plaster" (lit. "stone cream"), <i>shíhuī</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">石灰</span> "lime" (lit. "stone dust"), <i>shíkū</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">石窟</span> "grotto" (lit. "stone cave"), <i>shíyīng</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">石英</span> "quartz" (lit. "stone flower"), <i>shíyóu</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">石油</span> "petroleum" (lit. "stone oil").
</p><p>Most modern varieties of Chinese have the tendency to form new words through disyllabic, trisyllabic and tetra-character <a href="/wiki/Compound_word" class="mw-redirect" title="Compound word">compounds</a>. In some cases, monosyllabic words have become disyllabic without compounding, as in <i>kūlong</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">窟窿</span> from <i>kǒng</i> 孔; this is especially common in <a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">Jin</a>.
</p><p>Chinese <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> is strictly bound to a set number of <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllables</a> with a fairly rigid construction. Although many of these single-syllable morphemes (<i>zì</i>, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">字</span>) can stand alone as individual <a href="/wiki/Word_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Word (linguistics)">words</a>, they more often than not form multi-syllabic <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compounds</a>, known as <i>cí</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">词</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">詞</span>), which more closely resembles the traditional Western notion of a word. A Chinese <i>cí</i> ("word") can consist of more than one character-morpheme, usually two, but there can be three or more.
</p><p>For example:
</p>
<ul><li><i>yún</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">云</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">雲</span> – "cloud"</li>
<li><i>hànbǎobāo</i>, <i>hànbǎo</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">汉堡包</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">漢堡包</span>, <span lang="zh-Hans">汉堡</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">漢堡</span> – "hamburger"</li>
<li><i>wǒ</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">我</span> – "I, me"</li>
<li><i>rén</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">人</span> – "people, human, mankind"</li>
<li><i>dìqiú</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">地球</span> – "The Earth"</li>
<li><i>shǎndiàn</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">闪电</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">閃電</span> – "lightning"</li>
<li><i>mèng</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">梦</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">夢</span> – "dream"</li></ul>
<p>All varieties of modern Chinese are <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">analytic languages</a>, in that they depend on <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a> (word order and sentence structure) rather than <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a>—i.e., changes in form of a word—to indicate the word's function in a sentence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198810_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198810-77">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In other words, Chinese has very few <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_inflection" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammatical inflection">grammatical inflections</a>—it possesses no <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tenses</a>, no <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_voice" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammatical voice">voices</a>, no <a href="/wiki/Number" title="Number">numbers</a> (singular, plural; though there are plural markers, for example for personal pronouns), and only a few <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">articles</a> (i.e., equivalents to "the, a, an" in English).<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>They make heavy use of <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_particle" title="Grammatical particle">grammatical particles</a> to indicate <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">mood</a>. In Mandarin Chinese, this involves the use of particles like <i>le</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">了</span> (perfective), <i>hái</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">还</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">還</span> ("still"), <i>yǐjīng</i> <span lang="zh-Hans">已经</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">已經</span> ("already"), and so on.
</p><p>Chinese has a <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93verb%E2%80%93object" title="Subject–verb–object">subject–verb–object</a> <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a>, and like many other <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_East_Asia" title="Languages of East Asia">languages of East Asia</a>, makes frequent use of the <a href="/wiki/Topic%E2%80%93comment" class="mw-redirect" title="Topic–comment">topic–comment</a> construction to form sentences. Chinese also has an extensive system of <a href="/wiki/Classifier_(linguistics)" title="Classifier (linguistics)">classifiers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Measure_word" title="Measure word">measure words</a>, another trait shared with neighboring languages like <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>. Other notable grammatical features common to all the spoken varieties of Chinese include the use of <a href="/wiki/Serial_verb_construction" title="Serial verb construction">serial verb construction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pro-drop_language" title="Pro-drop language">pronoun dropping</a> and the related <a href="/wiki/Null_subject_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Null subject language">subject dropping</a>.
</p><p>Although the grammars of the spoken varieties share many traits, they do possess differences.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The entire Chinese character corpus since antiquity comprises well over 20,000 characters, of which only roughly 10,000 are now commonly in use. However Chinese characters should not be confused with Chinese words. Because most Chinese words are made up of two or more characters, there are many more Chinese words than characters. A more accurate equivalent for a Chinese character is the <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a>, as characters represent the smallest grammatical units with individual meanings in the Chinese language.
</p><p>Estimates of the total number of Chinese words and lexicalized phrases vary greatly. The <i><a href="/wiki/Hanyu_Da_Zidian" title="Hanyu Da Zidian">Hanyu Da Zidian</a></i>, a compendium of Chinese characters, includes 54,678 head entries for characters, including <a href="/wiki/Bone_oracle" class="mw-redirect" title="Bone oracle">bone oracle</a> versions. The <i><a href="/wiki/Zhonghua_Zihai" title="Zhonghua Zihai">Zhonghua Zihai</a></i> (1994) contains 85,568 head entries for character definitions, and is the largest reference work based purely on character and its literary variants. The <a href="/wiki/CC-CEDICT" class="mw-redirect" title="CC-CEDICT">CC-CEDICT</a> project (2010) contains 97,404 contemporary entries including idioms, technology terms and names of political figures, businesses and products. The 2009 version of the Webster's Digital Chinese Dictionary (WDCD),<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> based on <a href="/wiki/CC-CEDICT" class="mw-redirect" title="CC-CEDICT">CC-CEDICT</a>, contains over 84,000 entries.
</p><p>The most comprehensive pure linguistic Chinese-language dictionary, the 12-volume <i><a href="/wiki/Hanyu_Da_Cidian" title="Hanyu Da Cidian">Hanyu Da Cidian</a></i>, records more than 23,000 head Chinese characters and gives over 370,000 definitions. The 1999 revised <i><a href="/wiki/Cihai" title="Cihai">Cihai</a></i>, a multi-volume encyclopedic dictionary reference work, gives 122,836 vocabulary entry definitions under 19,485 Chinese characters, including proper names, phrases and common zoological, geographical, sociological, scientific and technical terms.
</p><p>The 7th (2016) edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Xiandai_Hanyu_Cidian" title="Xiandai Hanyu Cidian">Xiandai Hanyu Cidian</a></i>, an authoritative one-volume dictionary on modern standard Chinese language as used in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, has 13,000 head characters and defines 70,000 words.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loanwords">Loanwords</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Loanwords">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like any other language, Chinese has absorbed a sizable number of <a href="/wiki/Loanwords" class="mw-redirect" title="Loanwords">loanwords</a> from other cultures. Most Chinese words are formed out of native Chinese <a href="/wiki/Morphemes" class="mw-redirect" title="Morphemes">morphemes</a>, including words describing imported objects and ideas. However, direct phonetic borrowing of foreign words has gone on since ancient times.
</p><p>Some early <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> loanwords in Chinese have been proposed, notably <span lang="zh"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%9C%9C" class="extiw" title="wikt:蜜">蜜</a></span> <i>mì</i> "honey", <span lang="zh-Hans"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%8B%AE" class="extiw" title="wikt:狮">狮</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%8D%85" class="extiw" title="wikt:獅">獅</a></span> <i>shī</i> "lion," and perhaps also <span lang="zh-Hans"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%A9%AC" class="extiw" title="wikt:马">马</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%A6%AC" class="extiw" title="wikt:馬">馬</a></span> <i>mǎ</i> "horse", <span lang="zh-Hans"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%8C%AA" class="extiw" title="wikt:猪">猪</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%B1%AC" class="extiw" title="wikt:豬">豬</a></span> <i>zhū</i> "pig", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">犬</span> <i>quǎn</i> "dog", and <span lang="zh-Hans"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%B9%85" class="extiw" title="wikt:鹅">鹅</a></span>/<span lang="zh-Hant"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%B5%9D" class="extiw" title="wikt:鵝">鵝</a></span> <i>é</i> "goose".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup>
Ancient words borrowed from along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> since <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> include <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">葡萄</span> <i>pútáo</i> "<a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grape</a>", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">石榴</span> <i>shíliu</i>/<i>shíliú</i> "<a href="/wiki/Pomegranate" title="Pomegranate">pomegranate</a>" and <span lang="zh-Hans">狮子</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">獅子</span> <i>shīzi</i> "<a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a>". Some words were borrowed from Buddhist scriptures, including <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">佛</span> <i>Fó</i> "Buddha" and <span lang="zh-Hans">菩萨</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">菩薩</span> <i>Púsà</i> "bodhisattva." Other words came from nomadic peoples to the north, such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">胡同</span> <i>hútòng</i> "<a href="/wiki/Hutong" title="Hutong">hutong</a>". Words borrowed from the peoples along the Silk Road, such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">葡萄</span> "grape," generally have <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persian</a> etymologies. Buddhist terminology is generally derived from <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> or <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81li" class="mw-redirect" title="Pāli">Pāli</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical language">liturgical languages</a> of <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a>. Words borrowed from the nomadic tribes of the <a href="/wiki/Gobi" class="mw-redirect" title="Gobi">Gobi</a>, Mongolian or northeast regions generally have <a href="/wiki/Altaic_languages" title="Altaic languages">Altaic</a> etymologies, such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">琵琶</span> <i>pípá</i>, the Chinese lute, or <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">酪</span> <i>lào</i>/<i>luò</i> "cheese" or "yoghurt", but from exactly which source is not always clear.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKane2006161_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKane2006161-81">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_borrowings">Modern borrowings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Modern borrowings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Translation_of_neologisms_into_Chinese" title="Translation of neologisms into Chinese">Translation of neologisms into Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transcription_into_Chinese_characters" title="Transcription into Chinese characters">Transcription into Chinese characters</a></div>
<p>Modern neologisms are primarily translated into Chinese in one of three ways: free translation (<i><a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calque</a></i>, or by meaning), phonetic translation (by sound), or <a href="/wiki/Phono-semantic_matching" title="Phono-semantic matching">a combination of the two</a>. Today, it is much more common to use existing Chinese morphemes to coin new words in order to represent imported concepts, such as technical expressions and <a href="/wiki/International_scientific_vocabulary" title="International scientific vocabulary">international scientific vocabulary</a>. Any <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> etymologies are dropped and converted into the corresponding Chinese characters (for example, <i>anti-</i> typically becomes "<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">反</span>", literally <i>opposite</i>), making them more comprehensible for Chinese but introducing more difficulties in understanding foreign texts. For example, the word <i>telephone</i> was loaned phonetically as <span lang="zh-Hans">德律风</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">德律風</span> (Shanghainese: <i>télífon</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[təlɪfoŋ]</span>, Mandarin: <i>délǜfēng</i>) during the 1920s and widely used in Shanghai, but later <span lang="zh-Hans">电话</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">電話</span> <i>diànhuà</i> (lit. "electric speech"), built out of native Chinese morphemes, became prevalent (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">電話</span> is in fact from the Japanese <span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">電話</span> <i>denwa</i>; see below for more Japanese loans). Other examples include <span lang="zh-Hans">电视</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">電視</span> <i>diànshì</i> (lit. "electric vision") for television, <span lang="zh-Hans">电脑</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">電腦</span> <i>diànnǎo</i> (lit. "electric brain") for computer; <span lang="zh-Hans">手机</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">手機</span> <i>shǒujī</i> (lit. "hand machine") for mobile phone, <span lang="zh-Hans">蓝牙</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">藍牙</span> <i>lányá</i> (lit. "blue tooth") for <a href="/wiki/Bluetooth" title="Bluetooth">Bluetooth</a>, and <span lang="zh-Hans">网志</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">網誌</span> <i>wǎngzhì</i> (lit. "internet logbook") for blog in Hong Kong and Macau Cantonese. Occasionally half-transliteration, half-translation compromises are accepted, such as <span lang="zh-Hans">汉堡包</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">漢堡包</span> <i>hànbǎobāo</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">漢堡</span> <i>hànbǎo</i> "Hamburg" + <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">包</span> <i>bāo</i> "bun") for "hamburger". Sometimes translations are designed so that they sound like the original while incorporating Chinese morphemes (<a href="/wiki/Phono-semantic_matching" title="Phono-semantic matching">phono-semantic matching</a>), such as <span lang="zh-Hans">拖拉机</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">拖拉機</span> <i>tuōlājī</i> "tractor" (lit. "dragging-pulling machine"), or <span lang="zh-Hans">马利奥</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">馬利奧</span> Mǎlì'ào for the video game character <a href="/wiki/Mario" title="Mario">Mario</a>. This is often done for commercial purposes, for example <span lang="zh-Hans">奔腾</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">奔騰</span> <i>bēnténg</i> (lit. "dashing-leaping") for <a href="/wiki/Pentium_(brand)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentium (brand)">Pentium</a> and <span lang="zh-Hans">赛百味</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">賽百味</span> <i>Sàibǎiwèi</i> (lit. "better-than hundred tastes") for <a href="/wiki/Subway_(restaurant)" title="Subway (restaurant)">Subway restaurants</a>.
</p><p>Foreign words, mainly <a href="/wiki/Proper_nouns" class="mw-redirect" title="Proper nouns">proper nouns</a>, continue to enter the Chinese language by transcription according to their pronunciations. This is done by employing Chinese characters with similar pronunciations. For example, "Israel" becomes <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">以色列</span> <i>Yǐsèliè</i>, "Paris" becomes <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">巴黎</span> <i>Bālí</i>. A rather small number of direct transliterations have survived as common words, including <span lang="zh-Hans">沙发</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">沙發</span> <i>shāfā</i> "sofa", <span lang="zh-Hans">马达</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">馬達</span> <i>mǎdá</i> "motor", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">幽默</span> <i>yōumò</i> "humor", <span lang="zh-Hans">逻辑</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">邏輯</span> <i>luóji</i>/<i>luójí</i> "logic", <span lang="zh-Hans">时髦</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">時髦</span> <i>shímáo</i> "smart, fashionable", and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">歇斯底里</span> <i>xiēsīdǐlǐ</i> "hysterics". The bulk of these words were originally coined in the Shanghai dialect during the early 20th century and were later loaned into Mandarin, hence their pronunciations in Mandarin may be quite off from the English. For example, <span lang="zh-Hans">沙发</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">沙發</span> "sofa" and <span lang="zh-Hans">马达</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">馬達</span> "motor" in Shanghainese sound more like their English counterparts. Cantonese differs from Mandarin with some transliterations, such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">梳化</span> <i>so<sup>1</sup> faa<sup>3*2</sup></i> "sofa" and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">摩打</span> <i>mo<sup>1</sup> daa<sup>2</sup></i> "motor".
</p><p>Western foreign words representing Western concepts have influenced Chinese since the 20th century through transcription. From <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> came <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">芭蕾</span> <i>bālěi</i> "ballet" and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">香槟</span> <i>xiāngbīn</i>, "champagne"; from <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">咖啡</span> <i>kāfēi</i> "caffè". English influence is particularly pronounced. From early 20th century Shanghainese, many English words are borrowed, such as <span lang="zh-Hans">高尔夫</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">高爾夫</span> <i>gāoěrfū</i> "golf" and the above-mentioned <span lang="zh-Hans">沙发</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">沙發</span> <i>shāfā</i> "sofa". Later, the United States <a href="/wiki/Soft_power" title="Soft power">soft influences</a> gave rise to <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">迪斯科</span> <i>dísikē</i>/<i>dísīkē</i> "disco", <span lang="zh-Hans">可乐</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">可樂</span> <i>kělè</i> "cola", and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">迷你</span> <i>mínǐ</i> "mini [skirt]". Contemporary colloquial Cantonese has distinct loanwords from English, such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">卡通</span> <i>kaa<sup>1</sup> tung<small>1</small></i> "cartoon", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">基佬</span> <i>gei<sup>1</sup> lou<sup>2</sup></i> "gay people", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">的士</span> <i>dik<sup>1</sup> si<sup>6*2</sup></i> "taxi", and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">巴士</span> <i>baa<sup>1</sup> si<sup>6*2</sup></i> "bus". With the rising popularity of the Internet, there is a current vogue in China for coining English transliterations, for example, <span lang="zh-Hans">粉丝</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">粉絲</span> <i>fěnsī</i> "fans", <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">黑客</span> <i>hēikè</i> "hacker" (lit. "black guest"), and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">博客</span> <i>bókè</i> "blog". In Taiwan, some of these transliterations are different, such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">駭客</span> <i>hàikè</i> for "hacker" and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">部落格</span> <i>bùluògé</i> for "blog" (lit. "interconnected tribes").
</p><p>Another result of the English influence on Chinese is the appearance in Modern Chinese texts of so-called <span lang="zh-Hans">字母词</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">字母詞</span> <i>zìmǔcí</i> (lit. "lettered words") spelled with letters from the English alphabet. This has appeared in magazines, newspapers, on web sites, and on TV: <span lang="zh-Hans">三G手机</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">三G手機</span> "3rd generation cell phones" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">三</span> <i>sān</i> "three" + G "generation" + <span lang="zh-Hans">手机</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">手機</span> <i>shǒujī</i> "mobile phones"), <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">IT界</span> "IT circles" (IT "information technology" + <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">界</span> <i>jiè</i> "industry"), HSK (<i>Hànyǔ Shuǐpíng Kǎoshì</i>, <span lang="zh-Hans">汉语水平考试</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">漢語水平考試</span>), GB (<i>Guóbiāo</i>, <span lang="zh-Hans">国标</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">國標</span>), <span lang="zh-Hans">CIF价</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">CIF價</span> (CIF "Cost, Insurance, Freight" + <span lang="zh-Hans">价</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">價</span> <i>jià</i> "price"), <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">e家庭</span> "e-home" (e "electronic" + <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">家庭</span> <i>jiātíng</i> "home"), <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hans">W时代</span>/<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>&#58; <span lang="zh-Hant">W時代</span> "wireless era" (W "wireless" + <span lang="zh-Hans">时代</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">時代</span> <i>shídài</i> "era"), <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">TV族</span> "TV watchers" (TV "television" + <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">族</span> <i>zú</i> "social group; clan"), <span lang="zh-Hans">后РС时代</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">後PC時代</span> "post-PC era" (<span lang="zh-Hans">后</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">後</span> <i>hòu</i> "after/post-" + PC "personal computer" + <span lang="zh-Hans">时代</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">時代</span>), and so on.
</p><p>Since the 20th century, another source of words has been <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> using existing <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a> (Chinese characters used in Japanese). Japanese re-molded European concepts and inventions into <i><a href="/wiki/Wasei-kango" title="Wasei-kango">wasei-kango</a></i><span style="font-weight: normal">&#32;(<span lang="ja">和製漢語</span>, lit. "Japanese-made Chinese")</span>, and many of these words have been re-loaned into modern Chinese. Other terms were coined by the Japanese by giving new senses to existing Chinese terms or by referring to expressions used in classical Chinese literature. For example, <i>jīngjì</i> (<span lang="zh-Hans">经济</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">經濟</span>; <span lang="ja" title="Japanese language text">経済</span> <i>keizai</i> in Japanese), which in the original Chinese meant "the workings of the state", was narrowed to "economy" in Japanese; this narrowed definition was then re-imported into Chinese. As a result, these terms are virtually indistinguishable from native Chinese words: indeed, there is some dispute over some of these terms as to whether the Japanese or Chinese coined them first. As a result of this loaning, Chinese, Korean, Japanese, and Vietnamese share a corpus of linguistic terms describing modern terminology, paralleling the similar corpus of terms built from Greco-Latin and shared among European languages.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">Writing system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Writing system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Written_Chinese" title="Written Chinese">Written Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mainland_Chinese_Braille" title="Mainland Chinese Braille">Mainland Chinese Braille</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Braille" title="Taiwanese Braille">Taiwanese Braille</a></div>
<p>The Chinese <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a> centers on <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>, which are written within imaginary square blocks, traditionally arranged in vertical columns, read from top to bottom down a column, and right to left across columns. Chinese characters denote <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a> independent of phonetic change. Thus the character <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%80" class="extiw" title="wikt:一">一</a></span> ("one") is uttered <i>yī</i> in <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>, <i>yat<sup>1</sup></i> in <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> and <i>it</i> in <a href="/wiki/Hokkien_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Hokkien dialect">Hokkien</a> (form of Min). Vocabularies from different major Chinese variants have diverged, and colloquial nonstandard written Chinese often makes use of unique "dialectal characters", such as <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">冇</span> and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">係</span> for Cantonese and <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a>, which are considered archaic or unused in standard written Chinese.
</p><p>Written colloquial Cantonese has become quite popular in online <a href="/wiki/Chat_room" title="Chat room">chat rooms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Instant_messaging" title="Instant messaging">instant messaging</a> amongst Hong-Kongers and Cantonese-speakers elsewhere. It is considered highly informal, and does not extend to many formal occasions.
</p><p>The Chinese had no uniform phonetic transcription system until the mid-20th century, although enunciation patterns were recorded in early <a href="/wiki/Rime_book" class="mw-redirect" title="Rime book">rime books</a> and dictionaries. Early <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> translators, working in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pali" title="Pali">Pali</a>, were the first to attempt to describe the sounds and enunciation patterns of Chinese in a foreign language. After the 15th century, the efforts of Jesuits and Western court missionaries resulted in some rudimentary Latin transcription systems, based on the <a href="/wiki/Nanjing_Mandarin" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanjing Mandarin">Nanjing Mandarin</a> dialect.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a>, women in certain areas write their local language in <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BC_Shu" class="mw-redirect" title="Nü Shu">Nü Shu</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabary</a> derived from <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Dungan_language" title="Dungan language">Dungan language</a>, considered by many a dialect of Mandarin, is nowadays written in <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyrillic">Cyrillic</a>, and was previously written in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Dungan_people" title="Dungan people">Dungan people</a> are primarily Muslim and live mainly in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>; some of the related <a href="/wiki/Hui_people" title="Hui people">Hui people</a> also speak the language and live mainly in China.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_characters">Chinese characters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Chinese characters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:XingshuLantingxv.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/XingshuLantingxv.jpg/220px-XingshuLantingxv.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="322" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/XingshuLantingxv.jpg/330px-XingshuLantingxv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/XingshuLantingxv.jpg 2x" data-file-width="363" data-file-height="531" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:XingshuLantingxv.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Lantingji_Xu" title="Lantingji Xu">Preface to the Poems Composed at the Orchid Pavilion</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a>, written in <a href="/wiki/Semi-cursive_script" title="Semi-cursive script">semi-cursive style</a></div></div></div>
<p>Each Chinese character represents a monosyllabic Chinese word or morpheme. In 100 CE, the famed <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Xu_Shen" title="Xu Shen">Xu Shen</a> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_character_classification" title="Chinese character classification">classified characters</a> into six categories, namely <a href="/wiki/Pictograph" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictograph">pictographs</a>, simple <a href="/wiki/Ideograph" class="mw-redirect" title="Ideograph">ideographs</a>, compound ideographs, phonetic loans, phonetic compounds and derivative characters. Of these, only 4% were categorized as pictographs, including many of the simplest characters, such as <i>rén</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">人</span> (human), <i>rì</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">日</span> (sun), <i>shān</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">山</span> (mountain; hill), <i>shuǐ</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">水</span> (water). Between 80% and 90% were classified as phonetic compounds such as <i>chōng</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">沖</span> (pour), combining a phonetic component <i>zhōng</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">中</span> (middle) with a semantic <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_character)" class="mw-redirect" title="Radical (Chinese character)">radical</a> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">氵</span> (water). Almost all characters created since have been made using this format. The 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Dictionary" title="Kangxi Dictionary">Kangxi Dictionary</a> recognized 214 radicals.
</p><p>Modern characters are styled after the <a href="/wiki/Kaishu" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaishu">regular script</a>. Various other written styles are also used in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">Chinese calligraphy</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">seal script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cursive_script_(East_Asia)" title="Cursive script (East Asia)">cursive script</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a>. Calligraphy artists can write in traditional and simplified characters, but they tend to use traditional characters for traditional art.
</p><p>There are currently two systems for Chinese characters. The <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Traditional Chinese character">traditional system</a>, used in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> and Chinese speaking communities (except <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>) outside <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, takes its form from standardized character forms dating back to the late Han dynasty. The <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_character" class="mw-redirect" title="Simplified Chinese character">Simplified Chinese character</a> system, introduced by the People's Republic of China in 1954 to promote mass <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a>, simplifies most complex traditional <a href="/wiki/Glyph" title="Glyph">glyphs</a> to fewer strokes, many to common cursive <a href="/wiki/Shorthand" title="Shorthand">shorthand</a> variants.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, which has a large Chinese community, was the second nation to officially adopt simplified characters, although it has also become the <i>de facto</i> standard for younger ethnic Chinese in <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> provides the platform to practice reading these alternative systems, be it traditional or simplified.
</p><p>A well-educated Chinese reader today recognizes approximately 4,000 to 6,000 characters; approximately 3,000 characters are required to read a <a href="/wiki/Newspapers_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Newspapers of the People&#39;s Republic of China">Mainland newspaper</a>. The PRC government defines literacy amongst workers as a knowledge of 2,000 characters, though this would be only functional literacy. School-children typically learn around 2,000 characters whereas scholars may memorize up to 10,000.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> A large unabridged <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">dictionary</a>, like the Kangxi Dictionary, contains over 40,000 characters, including obscure, variant, rare, and archaic characters; fewer than a quarter of these characters are now commonly used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Romanization">Romanization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Romanization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Chinese" title="Romanization of Chinese">Romanization of Chinese</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gwoyu.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gwoyu.svg/100px-Gwoyu.svg.png" decoding="async" width="100" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gwoyu.svg/150px-Gwoyu.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Gwoyu.svg/200px-Gwoyu.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="660" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gwoyu.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"National language" (<span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">國語</span>/<span lang="zh-Hans" title="Chinese language text">国语</span>; <i>Guóyǔ</i>) written in Traditional and Simplified Chinese characters, followed by various romanizations.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Romanization" title="Romanization">Romanization</a> is the process of transcribing a language into the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>. There are many systems of romanization for the Chinese varieties, due to the lack of a native phonetic transcription until modern times. Chinese is first known to have been written in Latin characters by Western <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_China" title="Christianity in China">Christian missionaries</a> in the 16th century.
</p><p>Today the most common romanization standard for Standard Chinese is <i><a href="/wiki/Hanyu_Pinyin" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanyu Pinyin">Hanyu Pinyin</a></i>, often known simply as pinyin, introduced in 1956 by the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People&#39;s Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a>, and later adopted by <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. Pinyin is almost universally employed now for teaching standard spoken Chinese in schools and universities across <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>. Chinese parents also use Pinyin to teach their children the sounds and tones of new words. In school books that teach Chinese, the Pinyin romanization is often shown below a picture of the thing the word represents, with the Chinese character alongside.
</p><p>The second-most common romanization system, the <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>, was invented by Thomas Wade in 1859 and modified by Herbert Giles in 1892. As this system approximates the phonology of Mandarin Chinese into English consonants and vowels, i.e. it is an <a href="/wiki/Anglicization" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglicization">Anglicization</a>, it may be particularly helpful for beginner Chinese speakers of an English-speaking background. Wade–Giles was found in academic use in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, particularly before the 1980s, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language_romanization_in_Taiwan" title="Chinese language romanization in Taiwan">until 2009</a> was widely used in Taiwan.
</p><p>When used within European texts, the <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">tone</a> transcriptions in both pinyin and Wade–Giles are often left out for simplicity; Wade–Giles' extensive use of apostrophes is also usually omitted. Thus, most Western readers will be much more familiar with <i>Beijing</i> than they will be with <i>Běijīng</i> (pinyin), and with <i>Taipei</i> than <i>T'ai²-pei³</i> (Wade–Giles). This simplification presents syllables as homophones which really are none, and therefore exaggerates the number of homophones almost by a factor of four.
</p><p>Here are a few examples of <i>Hanyu Pinyin</i> and Wade–Giles, for comparison:
</p>

<p>Other systems of romanization for Chinese include <a href="/wiki/Gwoyeu_Romatzyh" title="Gwoyeu Romatzyh">Gwoyeu Romatzyh</a>, the French <a href="/wiki/EFEO_Chinese_transcription" title="EFEO Chinese transcription">EFEO</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Yale_romanization_of_Mandarin" title="Yale romanization of Mandarin">Yale system</a> (invented during WWII for U.S. troops), as well as separate systems for <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Min_Nan" class="mw-redirect" title="Min Nan">Min Nan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a>, and other Chinese varieties.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_phonetic_transcriptions">Other phonetic transcriptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other phonetic transcriptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chinese varieties have been phonetically transcribed into many other writing systems over the centuries. The <a href="/wiki/%27Phags-pa_script" class="mw-redirect" title="&#39;Phags-pa script">'Phags-pa script</a>, for example, has been very helpful in reconstructing the pronunciations of premodern forms of Chinese.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Zhuyin" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhuyin">Zhuyin</a> (colloquially <i>bopomofo</i>), a <a href="/wiki/Semi-syllabary" title="Semi-syllabary">semi-syllabary</a> is still widely used in Taiwan's <a href="/wiki/Elementary_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Elementary school">elementary schools</a> to aid standard pronunciation. Although zhuyin characters are reminiscent of <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> script, there is no source to substantiate the claim that Katakana was the basis for the zhuyin system. A comparison table of zhuyin to pinyin exists in the <a href="/wiki/Bopomofo#Comparison" title="Bopomofo">zhuyin article</a>. Syllables based on pinyin and zhuyin can also be compared by looking at the following articles:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Pinyin_table" title="Pinyin table">Pinyin table</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zhuyin_table" title="Zhuyin table">Zhuyin table</a></li></ul>
<p>There are also at least two systems of <a href="/wiki/Cyrillization" title="Cyrillization">cyrillization</a> for Chinese. The most widespread is the <a href="/wiki/Cyrillization_of_Chinese_from_Pinyin" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyrillization of Chinese from Pinyin">Palladius system</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_foreign_language">As a foreign language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: As a foreign language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_as_a_foreign_language" title="Chinese as a foreign language">Chinese as a foreign language</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG/300px-Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG" decoding="async" width="300" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG/450px-Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG/600px-Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG 2x" data-file-width="987" data-file-height="651" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Language_Training_at_CASA.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Yang Lingfu, former curator of the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_China" title="National Museum of China">National Museum of China</a>, giving Chinese language instruction at the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Affairs_Staging_Area" title="Civil Affairs Staging Area">Civil Affairs Staging Area</a> in 1945.</div></div></div>
<p>With the growing importance and influence of China's economy globally, <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin</a> instruction is gaining popularity in schools in the United States, and has become an increasingly popular subject of study amongst the young in the Western world, as in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1991 there were 2,000 foreign learners taking China's official <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Proficiency_Test" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Proficiency Test">Chinese Proficiency Test</a> (also known as HSK, comparable to the English <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge_ESOL_examination" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Cambridge ESOL examination">Cambridge Certificate</a>), while in 2005, the number of candidates had risen sharply to 117,660.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> By 2010, 750,000 people had taken the Chinese Proficiency Test. By 2017, 6.5 million candidates had taken the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Proficiency_Test" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Proficiency Test">Chinese Proficiency Test</a> of various kinds.
</p><p>According to the Modern Language Association, there were 550 elementary, junior high and senior high schools providing Chinese programs in the United States in 2015, which represented a 100% increase in two years. At the same time, enrollment in Chinese language classes at college level had an increase of 51% from 2002 to 2015. On the other hand, the American Council on the Teaching of Foreign Languages also had figures suggesting that 30,000 – 50,000 students were studying Chinese in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-85">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, more than half a million Chinese students pursued post-secondary education overseas, whereas 400,000 international students came to China for higher education. Tsinghua University hosted 35,000 students from 116 countries in the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the increase in demand for Chinese as a second language, there are 330 institutions teaching Chinese language globally according to the Chinese Ministry of Education. The establishment of Confucius Institutes, which are the public institutions affiliated with the Ministry of Education of China, aims at promoting Chinese language and culture as well as supporting Chinese teaching overseas. There were more than 480 Confucius Institutes worldwide as of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-85">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">No specific variety of Chinese is official in Hong Kong and Macau. Residents predominantly speak Cantonese and use <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese characters</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> regional standard. <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Mandarin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese characters</a> as the national standard are also used in some official and educational settings. The HK SAR Government promotes 兩文三語 [Bi-literacy (Chinese, English) and Tri-lingualism (Cantonese, Mandarin, English)], while the Macau SAR Government promotes 三文四語 [Tri-literacy (Chinese, Portuguese, English) and Quad-lingualism (Cantonese, Mandarin, Portuguese, English)], especially in public education.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Various examples include:
<ul><li>David Crystal, <i>The Cambridge Encyclopedia of Language</i> (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1987), p. 312. "The mutual unintelligibility of the varieties is the main ground for referring to them as separate languages."</li>
<li>Charles N. Li, Sandra A. Thompson. <i>Mandarin Chinese: A Functional Reference Grammar</i> (1989), p.&#160;2. "The Chinese language family is genetically classified as an independent branch of the Sino-Tibetan language family."</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFNorman1988">Norman (1988)</a>, p.&#160;1. "[...] the modern Chinese dialects are really more like a family of languages [...]"</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFDeFrancis1984">DeFrancis (1984)</a>, p.&#160;56. "To call Chinese a single language composed of dialects with varying degrees of difference is to mislead by minimizing disparities that according to Chao are as great as those between English and Dutch. To call Chinese a family of languages is to suggest extralinguistic differences that in fact do not exist and to overlook the unique linguistic situation that exists in China."</li></ul>
Linguists in China often use a formulation introduced by <a href="/wiki/Fu_Maoji" title="Fu Maoji">Fu Maoji</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_China" title="Encyclopedia of China">Encyclopedia of China</a></i>: “<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">汉语在语言系属分类中相当于一个语族的地位。</span>” ("In language classification, Chinese has a status equivalent to a language family.")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair199110,_21_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair199110,_21-4">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-DeFrancis_p42-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-DeFrancis_p42_71-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-DeFrancis_p42_71-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDeFrancis1984">DeFrancis (1984)</a>, p.&#160;42 counts Chinese as having 1,277 tonal syllables, and about 398 to 418 if tones are disregarded; he cites Jespersen, Otto (1928) <i>Monosyllabism in English</i>; London, p.&#160;15 for a count of over 8000 syllables for English.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Chao_p24-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Chao_p24_74-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A word pronounced in a wrong tone or inaccurate tone sounds as puzzling as if one said <i>bud</i> in English, meaning 'not good' or 'the thing one sleeps in.'"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChao194824_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChao194824-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A distinction is made between <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">他</span> as "he" and <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">她</span> as "she" in writing, but this is a 20th-century introduction, and both characters are pronounced in exactly the same way.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a> s.v. "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.britannica.com/eb/article-75039/Chinese-languages">Chinese languages</a>": "Old Chinese vocabulary already contained many words not generally occurring in the other Sino-Tibetan languages. The words for 'honey' and 'lion', and probably also 'horse', 'dog', and 'goose', are connected with Indo-European and were acquired through trade and early contacts. (The nearest known Indo-European languages were Tocharian and Sogdian, a middle Iranian language.) A number of words have Austroasiatic cognates and point to early contacts with the ancestral language of Muong–Vietnamese and Mon–Khmer."; Jan Ulenbrook, <i>Einige Übereinstimmungen zwischen dem Chinesischen und dem Indogermanischen</i> (1967) proposes 57 items; see also Tsung-tung Chang, 1988 <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://sino-platonic.org/complete/spp007_old_chinese.pdf">Indo-European Vocabulary in Old Chinese</a>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
